citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Lake+Victoria%22" rel="nofollow">"Lake Victoria"</a> – <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Lake+Victoria%22+-wikipedia&amp;tbs=ar:1" rel="nofollow">news</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Lake+Victoria%22&amp;tbs=bkt:s&amp;tbm=bks" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Lake+Victoria%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Lake+Victoria%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a> <b>·</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Lake+Victoria%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2017</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The only outflow from Lake Victoria is the Nile River, which exits the lake near Jinja, Uganda. In terms of contributed water, this makes Lake Victoria the principal source of the longest branch of the Nile. However, the most distal source of the Nile Basin, and therefore the ultimate <a href="/wiki/Nile#Sources" title="Nile">source of the Nile</a>, is more often considered to be one of the tributary rivers of the Kagera River (the exact tributary remains undetermined), and which originates in either <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a> or <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a>. The uppermost section of the Nile is generally known as the Victoria Nile until it reaches <a href="/wiki/Lake_Albert_(Africa)" title="Lake Albert (Africa)">Lake Albert</a>. Although it is a part of the same river system known as the <a href="/wiki/White_Nile" title="White Nile">White Nile</a> and is occasionally referred to as such, strictly speaking this name does not apply until after the river crosses the Uganda border into South Sudan to the north.
</p><p>The lake exhibits <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophic conditions</a>. In 1990–1991, oxygen concentrations in the mixed layer were higher than in 1960–1961, with nearly continuous oxygen supersaturation in surface waters. Oxygen concentrations in <a href="/wiki/Hypolimnion" title="Hypolimnion">hypolimnetic waters</a> (i.e. the layer of water that lies below the <a href="/wiki/Thermocline" title="Thermocline">thermocline</a>, is noncirculating, and remains perpetually cold) were lower in 1990–1991 for a longer period than in 1960–1961, with values of less than 1 mg per litre (&lt; 0.4 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grain_(mass)" title="Grain (mass)">gr</a>/cu ft) occurring in water as shallow as 40 metres (130 ft) compared with a shallowest occurrence of greater than 50 metres (160 ft) in 1961. The changes in oxygenation are considered consistent with measurements of higher algal biomass and productivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> These changes have arisen for multiple reasons: successive burning within its basin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> soot and ash from which has been deposited over the lake's wide area; from increased nutrient inflows via rivers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OchumbaKibaara_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-OchumbaKibaara-28">[28]</a></sup> and from increased pollution associated with settlement along its shores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 2010–2022 the surface area of Lake Victoria increased by 15%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> flooding lakeside communities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Bathymetry">Bathymetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Bathymetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lake_Vic_Bathy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3300" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Lake_Vic_Bathy.jpg/220px-Lake_Vic_Bathy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Lake_Vic_Bathy.jpg/330px-Lake_Vic_Bathy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Lake_Vic_Bathy.jpg/440px-Lake_Vic_Bathy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lake_Vic_Bathy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lake Victoria <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bathymetric" title="Bathymetric">bathymetric</a> model<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-32">[32]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The lake is considered a shallow lake considering its large geographic area with a maximum depth of approximately 80 metres (260 ft) and an average depth of 40 metres (130 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> A 2016 project digitized ten-thousand points and created the first true bathymetric map of the lake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-32">[32]</a></sup> The deepest part of the lake is offset to the east of the lake near Kenya and the lake is generally shallower in the west along the Ugandan shoreline and the south along the Tanzanian shoreline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Native_wildlife">Native wildlife</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Native wildlife">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mammals">Mammals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Mammals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many mammal species live in the region of Lake Victoria, and some of these are closely associated with the lake itself and the nearby wetlands. Among these are the <a href="/wiki/Hippopotamus" title="Hippopotamus">hippopotamus</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_clawless_otter" title="African clawless otter">African clawless otter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spotted-necked_otter" title="Spotted-necked otter">spotted-necked otter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marsh_mongoose" title="Marsh mongoose">marsh mongoose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sitatunga" title="Sitatunga">sitatunga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bohor_reedbuck" title="Bohor reedbuck">bohor reedbuck</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Defassa_waterbuck" title="Defassa waterbuck">defassa waterbuck</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cane_rat" title="Cane rat">cane rats</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Giant_otter_shrew" title="Giant otter shrew">giant otter shrew</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reptiles">Reptiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Reptiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lake Victoria and its wetlands has a large population of <a href="/wiki/Nile_crocodile" title="Nile crocodile">Nile crocodiles</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/African_helmeted_turtle" title="African helmeted turtle">African helmeted turtles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Variable_mud_turtle" title="Variable mud turtle">variable mud turtles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Williams%27_mud_turtle" title="Williams' mud turtle">Williams' mud turtle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spawls2002_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spawls2002-35">[35]</a></sup> The Williams' mud turtle is restricted to Lake Victoria and other lakes, rivers, and swamps in the upper Nile basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spawls2002_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Spawls2002-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cichlid_fish">Cichlid fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cichlid fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pundamilia_(Haplochromis)_nyererei_male.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Pundamilia_%28Haplochromis%29_nyererei_male.jpg/220px-Pundamilia_%28Haplochromis%29_nyererei_male.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Pundamilia_%28Haplochromis%29_nyererei_male.jpg/330px-Pundamilia_%28Haplochromis%29_nyererei_male.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Pundamilia_%28Haplochromis%29_nyererei_male.jpg/440px-Pundamilia_%28Haplochromis%29_nyererei_male.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pundamilia_(Haplochromis)_nyererei_male.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Unlike many other Lake Victoria cichlids, <i><a href="/wiki/Haplochromis_nyererei" title="Haplochromis nyererei">Haplochromis nyererei</a></i> remains common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Compared to several other cichlids, its eyes are particularly sensitive to light, especially red, which is less affected by the decrease in water clarity caused by <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a> than <a href="/wiki/Color#Physics_of_color" title="Color">short wavelength colors</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witte2000_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Witte2000-37">[37]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Lake Victoria formerly was very rich in fish, including many endemics, but a high percentage of these became extinct during the last 50 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witte1992_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Witte1992-38">[38]</a></sup> The main group in Lake Victoria is the <a href="/wiki/Haplochromine" title="Haplochromine">haplochromine</a> cichlids (<i><a href="/wiki/Haplochromis" title="Haplochromis">Haplochromis</a></i> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sensu_lato" title="Sensu lato">sensu lato</a></i>) with more than 500 species, almost all endemic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verheyen2003_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Verheyen2003-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meier2017_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meier2017-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeWeerdt2004_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeWeerdt2004-40">[40]</a></sup> and including an estimated 300 that still are <a href="/wiki/Undescribed_taxon" title="Undescribed taxon">undescribed</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sayer2018_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sayer2018-41">[41]</a></sup> This is far more species of fish than any other lake in the world, except <a href="/wiki/Lake_Malawi" title="Lake Malawi">Lake Malawi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner2001_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turner2001-42">[42]</a></sup> These are the result of a rapid <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_radiation" title="Adaptive radiation">adaptive radiation</a> in the last circa 15,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verheyen2003_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Verheyen2003-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meier2017_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Meier2017-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Their extraordinary diversity and speed of <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> have been the subjects for many scientists studying the forces that drive the richness of life everywhere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meier2017_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Meier2017-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup> The Victoria haplochromines are part of an older group of more than 700 closely related species, also including those of several smaller lakes in the region, notably <a href="/wiki/Lake_Kyoga" title="Lake Kyoga">Kyoga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Edward" title="Lake Edward">Edward</a>–<a href="/wiki/Lake_George_(Uganda)" title="Lake George (Uganda)">George</a>, Albert, and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Kivu" title="Lake Kivu">Kivu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verheyen2003_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-Verheyen2003-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meier2017_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-Meier2017-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of these lakes are relatively shallow (like Victoria) and part of the present-day upper Nile basin. The exception is Lake Kivu, which is part of the present-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congo_River_basin" title="Congo River basin">Congo River basin</a>, but is believed to have been connected to Lakes Edward and Victoria by rivers until the uplifting of parts of the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Rift" title="East African Rift">East African Rift</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verheyen2003_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-Verheyen2003-22">[22]</a></sup> This deep lake may have functioned as an "evolutionary reservoir" for this haplochromine group in periods where other shallower lakes in the region dried out, as happened to Lake Victoria about 15,000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verheyen2003_22-5"><a href="#cite_note-Verheyen2003-22">[22]</a></sup> In recent history only Lake Kyoga was easily accessible to Victoria cichlids, as further downstream movement by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victoria_Nile" title="Victoria Nile">Victoria Nile</a> (to Lake Albert) is prevented by a series of waterfalls, notably <a href="/wiki/Murchison_Falls" title="Murchison Falls">Murchison</a>. In contrast, the <a href="/wiki/Owen_Falls" title="Owen Falls">Owen Falls</a> (now flooded by a <a href="/wiki/Nalubaale_Hydroelectric_Power_Station" title="Nalubaale Hydroelectric Power Station">dam</a>) between Victoria and Kyoga were essentially a series of rapids that did not effectively block fish movements between the two lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Haplochromis_thereuterion.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="542" data-file-width="830" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Haplochromis_thereuterion.jpg/220px-Haplochromis_thereuterion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Haplochromis_thereuterion.jpg/330px-Haplochromis_thereuterion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Haplochromis_thereuterion.jpg/440px-Haplochromis_thereuterion.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Haplochromis_thereuterion.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Haplochromis_thereuterion&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Haplochromis thereuterion (page does not exist)">Haplochromis thereuterion</a></i> survives in low numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Initially feared extinct, when rediscovered it had changed habitat (from near surface to rocky outcrops) and feeding behavior (from surface insects to insect larvae)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The Victoria haplochromines are distinctly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sexually_dimorphic" title="Sexually dimorphic">sexually dimorphic</a> (males relatively brightly colored; females dull),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> and their ecology is extremely diverse, falling into at least 16 groups, including <a href="/wiki/Detritivore" title="Detritivore">detritivores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Planktivore" title="Planktivore">zooplanktivores</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insectivore" title="Insectivore">insectivores</a>, prawn-eaters, <a href="/wiki/Molluscivore" title="Molluscivore">molluscivores</a> and <a href="/wiki/Piscivore" title="Piscivore">piscivores</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup> As a result of predation by the introduced <a href="/wiki/Nile_perch" title="Nile perch">Nile perch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a> and other changes to the ecosystem, it is estimated that at least 200 species (about 40 percent) of Lake Victoria haplochromines have become extinct,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeWeerdt2004_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-DeWeerdt2004-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rijssel2013_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rijssel2013-49">[49]</a></sup> including more than 100 undescribed species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sayer2018_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sayer2018-41">[41]</a></sup> Initially it was feared that this number was even higher, by some estimates 65 percent of the total species,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> but several species that were feared extinct have been rediscovered after the Nile perch started to decline in the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCNef_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-IUCNef-51">[51]</a></sup> Several of the remaining species are seriously threatened and additional extinctions are possible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fiedler1998_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fiedler1998-52">[52]</a></sup> Some species have survived in nearby small satellite lakes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IUCNef_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-IUCNef-51">[51]</a></sup> have survived in refugias among rocks or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papyrus_sedge" title="Papyrus sedge">papyrus sedges</a> (protecting them from the Nile perch),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1996_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chapman1996-53">[53]</a></sup> or have adapted to the human-induced changes in the lake itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rijssel2013_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rijssel2013-49">[49]</a></sup> Such adaptions include a larger <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gill</a> area (adaption for oxygen-poor water), changes in the feeding apparatus, changes to the eyes (giving them a better sight in turbid water)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witte2000_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Witte2000-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-5"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup> and smaller head/larger <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caudal_peduncle" title="Caudal peduncle">caudal peduncle</a> (allowing faster swimming).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The piscivorous (affected by both predation and competition from Nile perch<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>), molluscivorous and insectivorous haplochromines were particularly hard hit with many extinctions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-6"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup> Others have become extinct in their pure form, but survive as <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrids</a> between close relatives (especially among the detritivores).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeWeerdt2004_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-DeWeerdt2004-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-7"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup> The zooplanktivores have been least affected and in the late 1990s had reached densities similar to, or above, the densities before the drastic declines, although consisting of fewer species and often switching their diet towards <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macroinvertebrate" title="Macroinvertebrate">macroinvertebrates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witte2000_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Witte2000-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-8"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup> Some of the threatened Lake Victoria cichlid species have captive "insurance" populations in zoos, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_aquaria" title="Public aquaria">public aquaria</a> and among private aquarists, and a few species are <a href="/wiki/Extinct_in_the_wild" title="Extinct in the wild">extinct in the wild</a> (only survive in captivity).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before the mass extinction that has occurred among the lake's cichlids in the last 50 years, about 90 percent of the native fish species in the lake were haplochromines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witte1992_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Witte1992-38">[38]</a></sup> Disregarding the haplochromines, the only native Victoria cichlids are two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critically_endangered" title="Critically endangered">critically endangered</a> tilapia, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Singida_tilapia" title="Singida tilapia">Singida tilapia</a> or <i>ngege</i> (<i>Oreochromis esculentus</i>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victoria_tilapia" title="Victoria tilapia">Victoria tilapia</a> (<i>O. variabilis</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1927–1928 <a href="/wiki/Michael_Graham_(scientist)" title="Michael Graham (scientist)">Michael Graham</a> conducted the first ever systematic <a href="/wiki/Fisheries_Survey_of_Lake_Victoria" title="Fisheries Survey of Lake Victoria">Fisheries Survey of Lake Victoria</a>. In his official report of the expedition, Graham wrote that "The ngege or satu <i>Tilapia esculenta</i>, is the most important food fish of the lake, whether for native or non-native consumption. No other fish equals it in the quality of the flesh. It is convenient size for trade, travels well and is found in much greater numbers than other important fish, such as semutundu (Luganda), <i>Bagrus sp.</i>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:25_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-:25-63">[63]</a></sup> Furthermore, Graham noted that the introduction of the European flax <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gill_net" title="Gill net">gill net</a> of 5 inch mesh had undoubtedly caused a diminution in the number of ngege in those parts of the Kavirondo Gulf, the northern shore of the lake, the Sesse Islands and Smith's Sound which are conveniently situated close to markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:25_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-:25-63">[63]</a></sup> Survey catches in 1927–28 included several <a href="/wiki/Haplochromis" title="Haplochromis">Haplochromis</a> species that are now thought to be extinct, including: <i><a href="/wiki/Haplochromis_flavipinnis" title="Haplochromis flavipinnis">Haplochromis flavipinnis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Haplochromis_gowersii" title="Haplochromis gowersii">Haplochromis gowersii</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Haplochromis_longirostris" title="Haplochromis longirostris">Haplochromis longirostris</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Haplochromis_macrognathus" title="Haplochromis macrognathus">Haplochromis macrognathus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Haplochromis_michaeli" title="Haplochromis michaeli">Haplochromis michaeli</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Haplochromis_nigrescens" title="Haplochromis nigrescens">Haplochromis nigrescens</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Haplochromis_prognathus" title="Haplochromis prognathus">Haplochromis prognathus</a></i>.
</p><p>As well as being due to the introduction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_Perch" title="Nile Perch">Nile Perch</a>, the extinction of cichlids in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Haplochromis" title="Haplochromis">Haplochromis</a></i> has also been blamed on the lake's eutrophication. The fertility of tropical waters depends on the rate at which nutrients can be brought into solution. The influent rivers of Lake Victoria provide few nutrients to the lake in relation to its size. Because of this, most of Lake Victoria's nutrients are thought to be locked up in lake-bottom deposits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hickling61_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hickling61-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beauchamp54_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beauchamp54-64">[64]</a></sup> By itself, this vegetative matter decays slowly. Animal flesh decays considerably faster, however, so the fertility of the lake is dependent on the rate at which these nutrients can be taken up by fish and other organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beauchamp54_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Beauchamp54-64">[64]</a></sup> There is little doubt that <i>Haplochromis</i> played an important role in returning detritus and plankton back into solution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lucy_Richardson_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lucy_Richardson-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KaufmanOchumba93_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-KaufmanOchumba93-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldschmidt93_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldschmidt93-67">[67]</a></sup> With some 80 percent of <i>Haplochromis</i> species feeding off detritus, and equally capable of feeding off one another, they represented a tight, internal recycling system, moving nutrients and biomass both vertically and horizontally through the water column, and even out of the lake via predation by humans and terrestrial animals. The removal of <i>Haplochromis</i>, however, may have contributed to the increasing frequency of <a href="/wiki/Algal_bloom" title="Algal bloom">algal blooms</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OchumbaKibaara_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-OchumbaKibaara-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KaufmanOchumba93_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-KaufmanOchumba93-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldschmidt93_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Goldschmidt93-67">[67]</a></sup> which may in turn be responsible for mass <a href="/wiki/Fish_kill" title="Fish kill">fish kills</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OchumbaKibaara_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-OchumbaKibaara-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_fish">Other fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Other fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The non-cichlid native fish include African tetras (<i><a href="/wiki/Brycinus" title="Brycinus">Brycinus</a></i>), cyprinids (<i><a href="/wiki/Enteromius" title="Enteromius">Enteromius</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Garra" title="Garra">Garra</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Labeo" title="Labeo">Labeo</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Labeobarbus" title="Labeobarbus">Labeobarbus</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rastrineobola" title="Rastrineobola">Rastrineobola</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xenobarbus" title="Xenobarbus">Xenobarbus</a></i>), airbreathing catfish (<i><a href="/wiki/Clariallabes" title="Clariallabes">Clariallabes</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Clarias" title="Clarias">Clarias</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xenoclarias" title="Xenoclarias">Xenoclarias</a></i>), bagrid catfish (<i><a href="/wiki/Bagrus" title="Bagrus">Bagrus</a></i>), loach catfish (<i><a href="/wiki/Amphilius" title="Amphilius">Amphilius</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Zaireichthys" title="Zaireichthys">Zaireichthys</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Schilbe_intermedius" title="Schilbe intermedius">silver butter catfish</a> (<i>Schilbe intermedius</i>), <i><a href="/wiki/Synodontis" title="Synodontis">Synodontis</a></i> squeaker catfish, <i><a href="/wiki/Nothobranchius" title="Nothobranchius">Nothobranchius</a></i> killifish, poeciliids (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aplocheilichthys" title="Aplocheilichthys">Aplocheilichthys</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Micropanchax" title="Micropanchax">Micropanchax</a></i>), the spiny eel <i><a href="/wiki/Mastacembelus" title="Mastacembelus">Mastacembelus</a> frenatus</i>, elephantfish (<i><a href="/wiki/Gnathonemus" title="Gnathonemus">Gnathonemus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hippopotamyrus" title="Hippopotamyrus">Hippopotamyrus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Marcusenius" title="Marcusenius">Marcusenius</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Mormyrus" title="Mormyrus">Mormyrus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Petrocephalus" title="Petrocephalus">Petrocephalus</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Pollimyrus" title="Pollimyrus">Pollimyrus</a></i>), the climbing gourami <i><a href="/wiki/Ctenopoma" title="Ctenopoma">Ctenopoma</a> muriei</i> and <a href="/wiki/Marbled_lungfish" title="Marbled lungfish">marbled lungfish</a> (<i>Protopterus aethiopicus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FishBaseList_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FishBaseList-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>At a genus level, most of these are widespread in Africa, but the very rare <i>Xenobarbus</i> and <i>Xenoclarias</i> are endemic to the lake, and the common <i>Rastrineobola</i> is near-endemic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FishBaseList_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-FishBaseList-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crustaceans">Crustaceans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Crustaceans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Four species of <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_crab" title="Freshwater crab">freshwater crabs</a> are known from Lake Victoria: <i><a href="/wiki/Potamonautes_niloticus" title="Potamonautes niloticus">Potamonautes niloticus</a></i> is widespread in the lake and <i><a href="/wiki/Potamonautes_emini" title="Potamonautes emini">P. emini</a></i> has been recorded from the vicinity of <a href="/wiki/Bukoba" title="Bukoba">Bukoba</a> in Tanzania, but both are also found elsewhere in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The last were first scientifically described in 2017 and very little is known about them: <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Potamonautes_entebbe&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Potamonautes entebbe (page does not exist)">P. entebbe</a></i> is only known from near <a href="/wiki/Entebbe" title="Entebbe">Entebbe</a> (the only known specimen was collected in 1955 and it is unknown if it was in or near the lake) and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Potamonautes_busungwe&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Potamonautes busungwe (page does not exist)">P. busungwe</a></i> only at Busungwe Island in the northwestern part of the lake. The latter likely is the smallest African freshwater crab with a carapace width up to about 1.6 cm (0.6 in), although <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Potamonautes_kantsyore&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Potamonautes kantsyore (page does not exist)">P. kantsyore</a></i> of <a href="/wiki/Kagera_River" title="Kagera River">Kagera River</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Platythelphusa" title="Platythelphusa">Platythelphusa</a> maculata</i> and <i>P. polita</i> of Lake Tanganyika are almost as small.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>The only shrimp/prawn is <i><a href="/wiki/Caridina_nilotica" title="Caridina nilotica">Caridina nilotica</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> which is common and widespread in Lake Victoria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-9"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Molluscs">Molluscs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Molluscs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Lake Victoria is home to 28 species of <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_snail" title="Freshwater snail">freshwater snails</a> (e.g., <i><a href="/wiki/Bellamya_(gastropod)" title="Bellamya (gastropod)">Bellamya</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Biomphalaria" title="Biomphalaria">Biomphalaria</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Bulinus" title="Bulinus">Bulinus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Cleopatra_(gastropod)" title="Cleopatra (gastropod)">Cleopatra</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Gabbiella" title="Gabbiella">Gabbiella</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Melanoides" title="Melanoides">Melanoides</a></i>), including 12 endemic species/subspecies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Darwall2011_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Darwall2011-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> There are 17 species of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bivalve" title="Bivalve">bivalves</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Corbicula" title="Corbicula">Corbicula</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Coelatura" title="Coelatura">Coelatura</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sphaerium" title="Sphaerium">Sphaerium</a></i>, and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Byssanodonta&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Byssanodonta (page does not exist)">Byssanodonta</a></i>), including 6 endemic species/subspecies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Darwall2011_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Darwall2011-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> It is likely that undescribed species of snails remain. Conversely, <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> studies indicate that some <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphologically</a> distinctive populations, traditionally regarded as separate species, may only be variants of single species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sayer2018_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sayer2018-41">[41]</a></sup> Two of the snail genera, <i>Biomphalaria</i> and  <i>Bulinus</i>,  are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intermediate_host" title="Intermediate host">intermediate hosts</a> of the parasite that causes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bilharzia" title="Bilharzia">bilharzia</a> (schistosomiasis). Human infections by this parasite are common at Lake Victoria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> This may increase as a result of the spread of the invasive water hyacinth (an optimum snail habitat),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> and the loss of many snail-eating cichlids in the lake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spiders">Spiders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Spiders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Evarcha_culicivora" title="Evarcha culicivora">Evarcha culicivora</a></i> is a species of jumping spider (family <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salticidae" title="Salticidae">Salticidae</a>) found only around Lake Victoria in Kenya and Uganda. It feeds primarily on female mosquitos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fisheries">Fisheries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fisheries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ugandan_fishing_boats.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Ugandan_fishing_boats.jpg/220px-Ugandan_fishing_boats.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Ugandan_fishing_boats.jpg/330px-Ugandan_fishing_boats.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Ugandan_fishing_boats.jpg/440px-Ugandan_fishing_boats.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ugandan_fishing_boats.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fishers and their boats on the shore of Lake Victoria</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fishing_on_Lake_Victoria" title="Fishing on Lake Victoria">Fishing on Lake Victoria</a></div>
<p>Lake Victoria supports Africa's largest inland <a href="/wiki/Fishery" title="Fishery">fishery</a> (as of 1997).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geheb97_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geheb97-80">[80]</a></sup> Initially the fishery involved native species, especially tilapia and haplochromine cichlids, but also catfish (<i>Bagrus</i>, <i>Clarias</i>, <i>Synodontis</i> and silver butter catfish), elephantfish, <a href="/wiki/Ningu" title="Ningu">ningu</a> (<i>Labeo victorianus</i>) and marbled lungfish (<i>Protopterus aethiopicus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LVFO2016_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-LVFO2016-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Njiru2005_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Njiru2005-82">[82]</a></sup> Some of these, including tilapia and ningu (<i>Labeo victorianus</i>), had already declined in the first half of the 20th century due to <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-10"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> To boost fishing, several species of non-native tilapia and Nile perch were introduced to the lake in the 1950s. Nevertheless, the natives continued to dominate fisheries until the 1970s where their decline meant that there was a strong shift towards the non-native <a href="/wiki/Nile_tilapia" title="Nile tilapia">Nile tilapia</a> (now 7 percent of catches), non-native Nile perch (60 percent) and the native <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria_sardine" title="Lake Victoria sardine">Lake Victoria sardine</a> (30 percent).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-11"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Njiru2005_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Njiru2005-82">[82]</a></sup> Because of its small size, the abundant open-water Lake Victoria sardine only supported minor fisheries until the decline of other natives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Njiru2005_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-Njiru2005-82">[82]</a></sup> At the peak in the early 1990s, 500,000 tonnes (490,000 long tons; 550,000 short tons) of Nile perch were landed annually in Lake Victoria, but this has declined significantly in later years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-12"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_issues">Environmental issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Environmental issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_issue" title="Environmental issue">environmental issues</a> are associated with Lake Victoria and the complete disappearance of many endemic cichlid species has been called the "most dramatic example of human-caused extinctions within an ecosystem".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fiedler1998_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fiedler1998-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Invasive_fish">Invasive fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Invasive fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Starting in the 1950s, many species have been <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introduced</a> to Lake Victoria where they have become <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive</a> and a prime reason for the extinction of many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endemic" title="Endemic">endemic</a> <a href="/wiki/Haplochromine" title="Haplochromine">haplochromine</a> cichlids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witte1992_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Witte1992-38">[38]</a></sup> Among the introductions are several <a href="/wiki/Tilapia" title="Tilapia">tilapias</a>: <a href="/wiki/Redbreast_tilapia" title="Redbreast tilapia">redbreast</a> (<i>Coptodon rendalli</i>), <a href="/wiki/Redbelly_tilapia" title="Redbelly tilapia">redbelly</a> (<i>C. zillii</i>),  Nile (<i>Oreochromis niloticus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Oreochromis_leucostictus" title="Oreochromis leucostictus">blue-spotted tilapias</a> (<i>O. leucostictus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-13"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Njiru2005_82-3"><a href="#cite_note-Njiru2005-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pringle2005_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pringle2005-84">[84]</a></sup> Although these have contributed to the extinction of native fish by causing significant changes to the <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a>, outcompeted natives and (in the case of the Nile tilapia) possibly hybridized with the highly threatened native tilapias, the most infamous introduction was the large and highly predatory <a href="/wiki/Nile_perch" title="Nile perch">Nile perch</a> (<i>Lates niloticus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Witte1992_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-Witte1992-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-14"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Njiru2005_82-4"><a href="#cite_note-Njiru2005-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lates_niloticus_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1054" data-file-width="487" decoding="async" height="368" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Lates_niloticus_2.jpg/170px-Lates_niloticus_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Lates_niloticus_2.jpg/255px-Lates_niloticus_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Lates_niloticus_2.jpg/340px-Lates_niloticus_2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lates_niloticus_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Nile_perch" title="Nile perch">Nile perch</a> was introduced to Lake Victoria for fishing, and can reach up to 2 m (6.6 ft) and 200 kg (440 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>As early as the 1920s, it was proposed to introduce a large pelagic predator such as the Nile perch to improve the fisheries in the lake. At the same time it was warned that this could present a serious danger to the native fish species and required extensive research into possible ecological effects before done.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pringle2005_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pringle2005-84">[84]</a></sup> These warnings primarily concerned the native tilapia <i>O. esculentus</i>, as the smaller haplochromine cichlids (despite playing an important role in local fisheries) were regarded as "trash fish" by the colonial government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pringle2005_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-Pringle2005-84">[84]</a></sup> In the following decades, the pressure to introduce the Nile perch continued, as did warnings about the possible effects of doing it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pringle2005_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-Pringle2005-84">[84]</a></sup> The first introduction of Nile perch to the region, done by the <a href="/wiki/Uganda_Game_and_Fisheries_Department" title="Uganda Game and Fisheries Department">Uganda Game and Fisheries Department</a> (then part of the colonial government) and local African fish guards, happened upstream of Murchison Falls directly after the completion of the Owen Falls Dam in 1954. This allowed it to spread to Lake Kyoga where additional Nile perch were released in 1955, but not Victoria itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pringle2005_84-4"><a href="#cite_note-Pringle2005-84">[84]</a></sup> Scientists argued that further introduction should wait until research showed the effect of the introduction in Kyoga, but by the late 1950s, Nile perch began being caught in Lake Victoria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pringle2005_84-5"><a href="#cite_note-Pringle2005-84">[84]</a></sup> As the species was already present, there were few objections when more Nile perch were transferred to Victoria to further bolster the stock in 1962–63.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pringle2005_84-6"><a href="#cite_note-Pringle2005-84">[84]</a></sup> The origin of the first Victoria introductions in the 1950s is not entirely clear and indisputable evidence is lacking. Uganda Game and Fisheries Department (UGFD) officials denied that they were involved, but circumstantial evidence suggests otherwise and local Africans employed by UGFD have said that they introduced the species in 1954–55 under the directive of senior officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pringle2005_84-7"><a href="#cite_note-Pringle2005-84">[84]</a></sup> UGFD officials argued that Nile perch must have spread to Lake Victoria by themselves by passing through the Owen Falls Dam when shut down for maintenance, but this is considered highly unlikely by many scientists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pringle2005_84-8"><a href="#cite_note-Pringle2005-84">[84]</a></sup> The Nile perch had spread throughout the lake by 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-15"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup> Initially the population of the Nile perch was relatively low, but a drastic increase happened, peaking in the 1980s, followed by a decline starting in the 1990s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lowe2009_44-16"><a href="#cite_note-Lowe2009-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to the presence of the Nile perch, the natural balance of the lake's ecosystem has been disrupted. The food chain is being altered and in some cases, broken by the indiscriminate eating habits of the Nile perch.
The subsequent decrease in the number of algae-eating fish allows the algae to grow at an alarming rate, thereby choking the lake. The increasing amounts of algae, in turn, increase the amount of detritus (dead plant material) that falls to the deeper portions of the lake before decomposing. As a by-product of this the oxygen levels in the deeper layer of water are being depleted. Without oxygen, any aerobic life (such as fish) cannot exist in the deeper parts of the lake, forcing all life to exist within a narrow range of depth. In this way, the Nile perch has degraded the diverse and thriving ecosystem that was once Lake Victoria. The abundance of aquatic life is not the only dependent of the lake: more than thirty million people in Tanzania, Kenya and Uganda rely on the lake for its natural resources.
</p><p>Hundreds of endemic species that evolved under the special conditions offered by the protection of Lake Victoria have been lost due to extinction, and several more are still threatened.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Their loss is devastating for the lake, the fields of ecology, genetics and evolution biology, and more evidently, for the local fisheries. Local fisheries once depended on catching the lungfish, tilapia, carp and catfish that comprise the local diet.
Today, the composition and yields of such fish catches are virtually negligible. Extensive fish kills, Nile perch, loss of habitat and overfishing have caused many fisheries to collapse and many protein sources to be unavailable at the market for local consumption. Few fisheries, though, have been able to make the switch to catching the Nile perch, since that requires a significant amount of capital resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_hyacinth_invasion">Water hyacinth invasion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Water hyacinth invasion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Water_hyacinth_in_Lake_Victoria" title="Water hyacinth in Lake Victoria">Water hyacinth in Lake Victoria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NdereIsland3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/NdereIsland3.jpg/220px-NdereIsland3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/NdereIsland3.jpg/330px-NdereIsland3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/NdereIsland3.jpg/440px-NdereIsland3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NdereIsland3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_hyacinth" title="Water hyacinth">hyacinth</a>-choked lakeshore at <a href="/wiki/Ndere_Island" title="Ndere Island">Ndere Island</a>, Lake Victoria, <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_hyacinth" title="Water hyacinth">water hyacinth</a> has become a major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invasive_plant_species" title="Invasive plant species">invasive plant species</a> in Lake Victoria.
</p><p>The release of large amounts of untreated wastewater (sewage) and agricultural and industrial runoff directly into Lake Victoria over the past 30 years has greatly increased the nutrient levels of nitrogen and phosphorus in the lake "triggering massive growth of exotic water hyacinth, which colonised the lake in the late 1990s".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luilo_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Luilo-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> This invasive weed creates anoxic (total depletion of oxygen levels) conditions in the lake inhibiting decomposing plant material, raising toxicity and disease levels to both fish and people. At the same time, the plant's mat or "web" creates a barrier for boats and ferries to maneuver, impedes access to the shoreline, interferes with hydroelectric power generation, and blocks the intake of water for industries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Luilo_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Luilo-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kateregga,_E._&amp;_Sterner,_T._90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kateregga,_E._&amp;_Sterner,_T.-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Albright2004_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Albright2004-93">[93]</a></sup> On the other hand, water hyacinth mats can potentially have a positive effect on fish life in that they create a barrier to overfishing and allow for fish growth, there has even been the reappearance of some fish species thought to have been extinct in recent years. The overall effects of the water hyacinth, however, are still unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kateregga,_E._&amp;_Sterner,_T._90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kateregga,_E._&amp;_Sterner,_T.-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Growth of the water hyacinth in Lake Victoria has been tracked since 1993, reaching its maxima biomass in 1997 and then declining again by the end of 2001.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kateregga,_E._&amp;_Sterner,_T._90-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kateregga,_E._&amp;_Sterner,_T.-90">[90]</a></sup> Greater growth was observed in the northern part of the lake, in relatively protected areas, which may be linked to current and weather patterns and could also be due to the climate and water conditions, which are more suitable to the plants growth (as there are large urban areas to the north end of the lake, in Uganda).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Albright2004_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-Albright2004-93">[93]</a></sup> The invasive weed was first attempted to be controlled by hand, removed manually from the lake; however, re-growth occurred quickly. Public awareness exercises were also conducted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Albright2004_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-Albright2004-93">[93]</a></sup> More recently, measures have been used such as the introduction of natural insect predators, including two different water hyacinth weevils and large harvesting and chopping boats, which seem to be much more effective in eliminating the water hyacinth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Albright2004_93-3"><a href="#cite_note-Albright2004-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> A green power plant that uses harvested water hyacinth (but also can use other degradable waste) was constructed in <a href="/wiki/Kisumu_County" title="Kisumu County">Kisumu County</a> in 2013. In addition to the <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a> it produces, its by-product can be used as <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sayer2018_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sayer2018-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other factors which may have contributed to the decline of the water hyacinth in Lake Victoria include varying weather patterns, such as El Niño during the last few months of 1997 and first six months of 1998 bringing with it higher levels of water in the lake and thus dislodging the plants. Heavy winds and rains along with their subsequent waves may have also damaged the plants during this same time frame. The plants may not have been destroyed, instead merely moved to another location. Additionally, the water quality, nutrient supply, temperature, and other environmental factors could have played a role. Overall, the timing of the decline could be linked to all of these factors and perhaps together, in combination, they were more effective than any one deterrent would have been by itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Albright2004_93-4"><a href="#cite_note-Albright2004-93">[93]</a></sup> The water hyacinth is in remission and this trend could be permanent if control efforts are continued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution">Pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Density_evolution_Victoria.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2347" data-file-width="3139" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Density_evolution_Victoria.png/280px-Density_evolution_Victoria.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Density_evolution_Victoria.png/420px-Density_evolution_Victoria.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Density_evolution_Victoria.png/560px-Density_evolution_Victoria.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Density_evolution_Victoria.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Population density around Lake Victoria</div></div></div>
<p>Pollution of Lake Victoria is mainly due to discharge of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raw_sewage" title="Raw sewage">raw sewage</a> into the lake, dumping of domestic and industrial waste, and fertiliser and chemicals from farms.
</p><p>The Lake Victoria basin, while generally rural, has many major centres of population. Its shores are dotted with key cities and towns, including <a href="/wiki/Kisumu" title="Kisumu">Kisumu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kisii,_Kenya" title="Kisii, Kenya">Kisii</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Homa_Bay" title="Homa Bay">Homa Bay</a> in Kenya; <a href="/wiki/Kampala" title="Kampala">Kampala</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jinja,_Uganda" title="Jinja, Uganda">Jinja</a> and Entebbe in Uganda; and Bukoba, <a href="/wiki/Mwanza" title="Mwanza">Mwanza</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Musoma" title="Musoma">Musoma</a> in Tanzania. These cities and towns are also home to many factories that discharge some chemicals directly into the lake or its influent rivers. The set up of small beaches and local authorities around the lake lack proper sewage treatment facilities allowing pollutants to find their way into the water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>  Large parts of these urban areas also discharge untreated (raw) sewage into the river, increasing its <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a> that in turn is helping to increase the invasive water hyacinth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-earthobservatory.nasa.gov_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-earthobservatory.nasa.gov-101">[101]</a></sup> Increased <a href="/wiki/Logging" title="Logging">logging</a> and act of <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> has led to environmental degradation around the region reducing the absorption of polluting chemicals and deteriorating the water quality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_data">Environmental data</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Environmental data">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of 2016, an environmental data repository exists for Lake Victoria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The repository contains shoreline, bathymetry, pollution, temperature, wind vector, and other important data for both the lake and the wider Basin.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_exploration">History and exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: History and exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-3-183.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="388" data-file-width="518" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-3-183.jpg/220px-The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-3-183.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-3-183.jpg/330px-The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-3-183.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-3-183.jpg/440px-The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-3-183.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_National_Archives_UK_-_CO_1069-3-183.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bismarck Rock</div></div></div>
<p>The first recorded information about Lake Victoria comes from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> traders plying the inland routes in search of <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a>, other precious commodities, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave" title="Slave">slaves</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Africa_Lake_Victoria_10_006.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Africa_Lake_Victoria_10_006.jpg/220px-Africa_Lake_Victoria_10_006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Africa_Lake_Victoria_10_006.jpg/330px-Africa_Lake_Victoria_10_006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Africa_Lake_Victoria_10_006.jpg/440px-Africa_Lake_Victoria_10_006.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Africa_Lake_Victoria_10_006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The lake as it is visible from the shores of the Speke Resort in Kampala, Uganda</div></div></div>
<p>The lake existed and was known to many Africans in the catchment area long before it was sighted by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_ethnic_groups" title="European ethnic groups">European</a> in 1858 when the <a href="/wiki/British_people" title="British people">British</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_explorers" title="List of explorers">explorer</a> <a href="/wiki/John_Hanning_Speke" title="John Hanning Speke">John Hanning Speke</a> reached its southern shore while on his journey with <a href="/wiki/Richard_Francis_Burton" title="Richard Francis Burton">Richard Francis Burton</a> to explore central Africa and locate the Great Lakes. Believing he had found the source of the Nile on seeing this "vast expanse of open water" for the first time, Speke named the lake after Queen Victoria. Burton, who had been recovering from illness at the time and resting further south on the shores of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tanganyika" title="Lake Tanganyika">Lake Tanganyika</a>, was outraged that Speke claimed to have proved his discovery to have been the true source of the Nile River, which Burton regarded as still unsettled. A very public quarrel ensued, which not only sparked a great deal of intense debate within the scientific community of the day, but also much interest by other explorers keen to either confirm or refute Speke's discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DNB_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-DNB-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 1860s, the famous Scottish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Explorer" title="Explorer">explorer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionary</a> <a href="/wiki/David_Livingstone" title="David Livingstone">David Livingstone</a> failed in his attempt to verify Speke's discovery, instead pushing too far west and entering the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_Congo" title="River Congo">River Congo</a> system instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Ultimately, the <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Americans" title="Welsh Americans">Welsh-American</a> explorer <a href="/wiki/Henry_Morton_Stanley" title="Henry Morton Stanley">Henry Morton Stanley</a>, on an expedition funded by the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Herald" title="New York Herald">New York Herald</a></i> newspaper, confirmed the truth of Speke's discovery, circumnavigating the lake and reporting the great outflow at <a href="/wiki/Ripon_Falls" title="Ripon Falls">Ripon Falls</a> on the lake's northern shore.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nalubaale_Dam">Nalubaale Dam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Nalubaale Dam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nalubaale_Power_Station" title="Nalubaale Power Station">Nalubaale Power Station</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:From_top_of_Nalubaale_Power_Station.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/From_top_of_Nalubaale_Power_Station.jpg/220px-From_top_of_Nalubaale_Power_Station.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/From_top_of_Nalubaale_Power_Station.jpg/330px-From_top_of_Nalubaale_Power_Station.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/From_top_of_Nalubaale_Power_Station.jpg/440px-From_top_of_Nalubaale_Power_Station.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:From_top_of_Nalubaale_Power_Station.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Nalubaale_Hydroelectric_Power_Station" title="Nalubaale Hydroelectric Power Station">Nalubaale Hydroelectric Power Station</a> in <a href="/wiki/Njeru" title="Njeru">Njeru</a>, Uganda.</div></div></div>
<p>The only outflow for Lake Victoria is at Jinja, Uganda, where it forms the Victoria Nile. The water for at least 12,000 years had drained across a natural rock weir. In 1952, engineers acting for the government of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Uganda" title="British Uganda">Colonial Uganda</a> blasted out the weir and reservoir to replace it with an artificial barrage to control the level of the lake and reduce the gradual erosion of the rock weir. A standard for mimicking the old rate of outflow called the "agreed curve" was established, setting the maximum flow rate at 300 to 1,700 <a href="/wiki/Cubic_metre_per_second" title="Cubic metre per second">cubic metres per second</a> (392–2,224 <a href="/wiki/Cubic_yard" title="Cubic yard">cu yd</a>/sec) depending on the lake's water level.
</p><p>In 2002, Uganda completed a second <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroelectric" title="Hydroelectric">hydroelectric</a> complex in the area, the <a href="/wiki/Kiira_Hydroelectric_Power_Station" title="Kiira Hydroelectric Power Station">Kiira Hydroelectric Power Station</a>, with <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> assistance. By 2006, the water levels in Lake Victoria had reached an 80-year low, and Daniel Kull, an independent hydrologist living in <a href="/wiki/Nairobi" title="Nairobi">Nairobi</a>, Kenya, calculated that Uganda was releasing about twice as much water as is allowed under the agreement,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> and was primarily responsible for recent drops in the lake's level.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_use">Water use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Water use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many towns and cities are reliant on Victoria for their water supplies, for farming and other uses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lamadi_water_scheme">Lamadi water scheme</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Lamadi water scheme">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Lamadi water scheme is a water and sanitation project that serves Mwanza and the satellite towns of Lamadi, Misungwi, Magu, Bukoba, and Musoma on the bank of Lake Victoria.  European Investment Bank started the project in 2013 with the aim of protecting the environmental health of the lake, through improved water and sanitation to the towns whose pollution is part of the degradation of the lake. The project aims to provide safe drinking water for an estimated one million people and improved sanitation for 100 000 people. Sediment and suspended solids are filtered out using sand, which acts like a sieve. The water is then ready to be chlorinated or treated in another way. The sand filtration helps reduce water-borne diseases and is based on the use of the local environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport">Transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria_ferries" title="Lake Victoria ferries">Lake Victoria ferries</a></div>
<p>Since the 1900s, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Victoria_ferries" title="Lake Victoria ferries">Lake Victoria ferries</a> have been an important means of transport between Uganda, Tanzania, and Kenya. The main ports on the lake are Kisumu, Mwanza, Bukoba, <a href="/wiki/Entebbe" title="Entebbe">Entebbe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Port_Bell" title="Port Bell">Port Bell</a>, and Jinja. Until 1963, the fastest and newest ferry, <a href="/wiki/MV_Victoria_(1959)" title="MV Victoria (1959)">MV <i>Victoria</i></a>, was designated a <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail_Ship" title="Royal Mail Ship">Royal Mail Ship</a>. In 1966, <a href="/wiki/Train_ferry" title="Train ferry">train ferry</a> services between Kenya and Tanzania were established with the introduction of <a href="/wiki/MV_Uhuru" title="MV Uhuru">MV <i>Uhuru</i></a> and <a href="/wiki/MV_Umoja" title="MV Umoja">MV <i>Umoja</i></a>. The ferry <a href="/wiki/MV_Bukoba" title="MV Bukoba">MV <i>Bukoba</i></a> sank in the lake on 21 May 1996 with a loss of between 800 and 1,000 lives, making it one of Africa's worst maritime disasters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Another tragedy occurred recently on 20 September 2018 that involved the passagers ferry <a href="/wiki/Sinking_of_MV_Nyerere" title="Sinking of MV Nyerere">MV Nyerere</a> from <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> that caused the deaths of over 200 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Lake_Victoria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<ul>
<li><span><img alt="image" class="noviewer" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Walden_Pond%2C_Massachusetts_on_June_27%2C_2012.png/28px-Walden_Pond%2C_Massachusetts_on_June_27%2C_2012.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Walden_Pond%2C_Massachusetts_on_June_27%2C_2012.png/42px-Walden_Pond%2C_Massachusetts_on_June_27%2C_2012.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Walden_Pond%2C_Massachusetts_on_June_27%2C_2012.png/56px-Walden_Pond%2C_Massachusetts_on_June_27%2C_2012.png 2x" width="28"/></span><span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Lakes" title="Portal:Lakes">Lakes portal</a></span></li>

<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097025294">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:#f9f9f9}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1}}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox">
